<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="038b32e0bc874abdacd4bdc57d0c845d"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="038b32e0bc874abdacd4bdc57d0c845d">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '7bd72207-6974-4af0-b332-0be57b333b9e'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

                var session_id = '38E8BDBE5B9C85411D3674AC57660744';

                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }

                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d8118054" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p><b>The P2@P3 Na<sub>0.78</sub>Cu<sub>0.33−<i>x</i></sub></b><b>Zn</b><sub><i><b>x</b></i></sub><b>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> composite</b> induced by Zn doping with revealed redox behavior of Cu<sup>2+/3+</sup> is a promising cathode for Na ion batteries. Zn doping not only induces the formation of a P3 phase, which shows a distinct crystal and atomic arrangement with nanoscale thickness, but also offered a high and flat voltage profile, leading to improvement in both structural/electrochemical stability and humidity resistance.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/029c7472-68bc-4187-a6a5-d0dacc35ca52/anie201811882-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/029c7472-68bc-4187-a6a5-d0dacc35ca52/anie201811882-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2526a4d1-014a-46c9-a307-2ce6961aaeb3/anie201811882-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/029c7472-68bc-4187-a6a5-d0dacc35ca52/anie201811882-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d8118057" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Low-cost layered oxides free of Ni and Co are considered to be the most promising cathode materials for future sodium-ion batteries. Biphasic Na<sub>0.78</sub>Cu<sub>0.27</sub>Zn<sub>0.06</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> obtained via superficial atomic-scale P3 intergrowth with P2 phase induced by Zn doping, consisting of inexpensive transition metals, is a promising cathode for sodium-ion batteries. The P3 phase as a covering layer in this composite shows not only in excellent electrochemical performance but also its tolerance to moisture. The results indicate that partial Zn substitutes can effectively control biphase formation for improving the structural/electrochemical stability as well as the ionic diffusion coefficient. Based on in situ synchrotron X-ray diffraction coupled with electron-energy-loss spectroscopy, a possible Cu<sup>2+/3+</sup> redox reaction mechanism has now been revealed.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie201811882-sec-0001">
         
         <p>Recently, sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) have been considered a promising replacement for LIB counterparts owing to the abundant and cost-effective nature of sodium resources.<span><sup><a href="#anie201811882-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0001_R_d8118046e547" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> In particular, the Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> cathode has drawn scientists’ attention over the past decades.<span><sup><a href="#anie201811882-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0002_R_d8118046e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Chem. Mater.">2</a>-<a href="#anie201811882-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0004_R_d8118046e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4">4</a></sup></span> P2-type Na<sub>x</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> (0.46≤<i>x</i>≤0.83) with a wide range of sodium content seems especially attractive as a candidate for high-rate and long-life electrodes.<span><sup><a href="#anie201811882-bib-0005a" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0005a_R_d8118046e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5a - Chem. Mater.">5a</a></sup></span>–<span><sup><a href="#anie201811882-bib-0005c" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0005c_R_d8118046e565" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5c - Energy Environ. Sci.">5c</a></sup></span> Moreover, first-principle calculations have demonstrated that sodium ions in the P2 structure can diffuse directly between two face-sharing trigonal prismatic sites with a rare phase transition.<span><sup><a href="#anie201811882-bib-0004a" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0004a_R_d8118046e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4a - Nat. Mater.">4a</a>, <a href="#anie201811882-bib-0005d" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0005d_R_d8118046e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5d - J. Electrochem. Soc.">5d</a></sup></span>–<span><sup><a href="#anie201811882-bib-0005f" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0005f_R_d8118046e569" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5f - Energy Environ. Sci.">5f</a></sup></span> Fe- and Mn-containing P2 phase layered oxides have been of particular interest since these elements are abundant and nontoxic.<span><sup><a href="#anie201811882-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0002_R_d8118046e572" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Chem. Mater.">2</a>, <a href="#anie201811882-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0003_R_d8118046e572" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#anie201811882-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0006_R_d8118046e572" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Chem. Mater.">6</a>, <a href="#anie201811882-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0007_R_d8118046e572" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7">7</a>, <a href="#anie201811882-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0008_R_d8118046e572" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">8</a></sup></span> However, as demonstrated by Victor and co-workers, the Jahn–Teller effect exhibited by Fe<sup>IV</sup> can potentially induce structural strain and the insertion of water and carbonate ions.<span><sup><a href="#anie201811882-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0003_R_d8118046e576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#anie201811882-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0006_R_d8118046e576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Chem. Mater.">6</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Recently, the biphase synergy in NIBs has been well-illustrated by Guo et al. (layered P2/O3 composite) and Hou et al. (layered P3/P2 composite).<span><sup><a href="#anie201811882-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0009_R_d8118046e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Angew. Chem.">9</a>, <a href="#anie201811882-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0010_R_d8118046e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nanoscale">10</a></sup></span> However, Li, Ni, and Co are unfavorable for NIBs in the long run cost-wise, and biphase synergy has so far not been demonstrated with cheap elements. Hu and co-workers<span><sup><a href="#anie201811882-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0011_R_d8118046e582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chin. Phys. B">11</a></sup></span> reported the reversible Cu<sup>3+</sup>/Cu<sup>2+</sup> redox couple (67 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1C) with only half the price of nickel and one-tenth that of cobalt, which greatly demonstrated the importance of copper in NIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie201811882-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0012_R_d8118046e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Sci.">12</a>-<a href="#anie201811882-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0015_R_d8118046e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15">15</a></sup></span> However the redox behavior of Cu<sup>2+/3+</sup> in previous reports is inconsistent even for the same material, and little research has been conducted into it.</p>
         
         <p>It would therefore be interesting if we can correlate the biphase synergy with cost-effective transition metals. Herein, we designed a novel hydrostable P2@P3 Na<sub>0.78</sub>Cu<sub>0.27</sub>Zn<sub>0.06</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NCZM) composite. Zn doping induced the formation of P2@P3 intergrowth, leading to improvement not only in structural/electrochemical stability but also in humidity resistance. We found that the main electrochemical reaction of Cu<sup>2+/3+</sup> occurs below 3.8 V, and delivers a discharge capacity of 84 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (1C), a rate capability of 73 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (5C), and a capacity retention of 85 % (72 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) at 1C after 200 cycles.</p>
         
         <p>The pure P2 compound Na<sub>0.78</sub>Cu<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NCM) shows agglomerated secondary particles with homogeneously distributed elements (Supporting Information, Figure S1). As characterized by X-ray diffraction (XRD), it consists of a pure P2 phase with refined lattice parameters: <i>α</i>=<i>b</i>=2.9100(7) Å, <i>c</i>=11.0674(4) Å, and <i>V</i>=81.90(2) Å<sup>3</sup> for the space group <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i> (Supporting Information, Table S1). For NCZM composite, the major diffraction peaks can be well indexed to P2-type phase corresponding to PDF: 00-054-0894 (Supporting Information, Figure S2). There are several other weak peaks that can be identified as P3-type structure (PDF: 00-054-0839) with space group <i>R</i><img alt="urn:x-wiley:14337851:media:anie201811882:anie201811882-math-0001" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/4bb71d88-4cf4-4f68-8e31-6190ef975bfb/anie201811882-math-0001.png">
            <i>m</i>, which is validated by the enlarged diffraction peaks (Supporting Information, Figure S2c). The peaks at 27.2° and 28.4° are related to the Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy.<span><sup><a href="#anie201811882-bib-0016a" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0016a_R_d8118046e653" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16a - J. Power Sources">16a</a></sup></span>–<span><sup><a href="#anie201811882-bib-0016c" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0016c_R_d8118046e656" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16c - NPG Asia Mater.">16c</a></sup></span> To obtain more detailed information on the crystal structure, synchrotron X-ray powder diffraction was employed. Both space groups were used for the Rietveld refinement, as displayed in Figure <a href="#anie201811882-fig-0001">1</a> a. The calculated results successfully gave reliability-factors (weighted profile factors) of <i>R</i><sub>wp</sub>=5.3 %. The calculated lattice parameters were <i>α</i>=<i>b</i>=2.9092(8) Å, <i>c</i>=11.1564(2) Å and <i>V</i>=81.77(6) Å<sup>3</sup> for the P2 component and <i>α</i>=<i>b</i>=2.8568(5) Å, <i>c</i>=16.7722(3) Å and <i>V</i>=118.99(5) Å<sup>3</sup> for the P3 component (Supporting Information, Table S2). Compared with the calculated results, the lattice parameter <i>c</i>=11.0674(4) Å in NCM is less than the parameter <i>c</i>=11.1564(2) Å of P2 phase in NCZM. This major difference indicates enlarged interlayer spacing of P2 phase in NCZM, which can not only provide more open space for Na<sup>+</sup> migration but also activate more sodium ions for sodium-ion diffusion. An illustration of the layered P2 and P3 structures can be found in Figure <a href="#anie201811882-fig-0001">1</a> b. This unique layer morphology of the NCZM composite coexist with numerous primary nanocrystals, which can efficiently facilitate sodium ion transport owing to the short diffusion distance.<span><sup><a href="#anie201811882-bib-0016j" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0016j_R_d8118046e694" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16j - Adv. Funct. Mater.">16j</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie201811882-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f294b30a-092a-45bf-9124-bac8951e1910/anie201811882-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f294b30a-092a-45bf-9124-bac8951e1910/anie201811882-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fca2901d-0b81-4202-9064-f9c184708c41/anie201811882-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f294b30a-092a-45bf-9124-bac8951e1910/anie201811882-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie201811882-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.201811882" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) Rietveld refinement of synchrotron XRD pattern of NCZM. Inset: SEM images in different magnification. b) Illustrations for the corresponding crystalline structure of P2 (left) and P3 (right).</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To investigate the phase distribution of the NCZM sample, a dynamic secondary ion mass spectroscopy (SIMS) experiment was conducted (Figure <a href="#anie201811882-fig-0002">2</a> a,b). The concentration of sodium is increased in the initial 400 s, whereas Cu and Zn remain stable and Mn decreases slightly. Notably, higher sodium signal inside and lower signal on the outer layer can be seen clearly. About 300 s sputtering is needed for the Na signal to become stable, indicating the sodium concentration on the surface is lower than within. Such distribution may play a role in tuning the unique surface chemistry when compared with NCM composite (Supporting Information, Figure S3). The STEM-electron energy loss spectroscopy (EELS) technique was employed (Figure <a href="#anie201811882-fig-0002">2</a> c–f) to investigate the valence states of Mn, Cu, and Zn. The peak of Mn-L<sub>3</sub> increased slightly from 643.5 eV (inner side) to 644.0 eV (surface), indicating that the valence of Mn on the surface is higher than on the inside.<span><sup><a href="#anie201811882-bib-0016d" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0016d_R_d8118046e726" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16d - Microsc. Microanal.">16d</a></sup></span> The Cu and Zn L<sub>3</sub>-edges located at 937.9 and 1036.5 eV, respectively, both correspond to the valence state of 2+. XPS and XANES were to further confirm the valence states from the macro-perspective (Supporting Information, Figure S4). The adsorption edge position of NCZM and NCM are similar to the copper(II)-based reference sample. Mn K-edge spectra shows that both NCZM and NCM reflect the similar feature as tetravalent Mn-ion (MnO<sub>2</sub>). Comparing the pre-edge features of NCM visible in the higher energy region to those of NCZM indicates the average oxidation state of Mn is higher in NCM than in NCZM. Closer examination of the pre-edge feature revealed that Mn<sup>III</sup> and Mn<sup>IV</sup> are coexist in NCZM, while the valence of Mn in NCM is closer to Mn<sup>IV</sup>. This is in good agreement with the electrochemical data. Based on the above results, a three-dimensional (3D) structural model with corresponding Na-ion migration paths of the NCZM composite is shown in Figure <a href="#anie201811882-fig-0002">2</a> g.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie201811882-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0e2b2ab9-3485-4c14-95f7-9ae2d1403535/anie201811882-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0e2b2ab9-3485-4c14-95f7-9ae2d1403535/anie201811882-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/97e6c431-c6c9-4ec1-b26f-c8342028864d/anie201811882-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0e2b2ab9-3485-4c14-95f7-9ae2d1403535/anie201811882-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie201811882-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.201811882" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a),b) Elemental SIMS mapping (a) and sputter depth profiles (b) of NCZM. c)–f) Specific location for EELS signals (c), energy loss spectra of NCZM for Cu L<sub>2,3</sub> (d), Mn L<sub>2,3</sub> (e) and Zn L<sub>2,3</sub> (f). Blue/red/black curves correspond to the selected blue/red/black areas in (c). g) 3D structural model of the NCZM samples composed of P3 and P2 with corresponding Na-ion migration paths in different layered structures.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Na<sub>0.78</sub>Cu<sub>0.33−<i>x</i></sub>Zn<sub><i>x</i></sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i>=0.03, 0.06, 0.11, and 0.33) composites with different Zn contents were studied (Supporting Information, Figure S5). It showed that the P3 phase is highly related with partial Zn doping. Thus, we speculate that Zn doping in the transition metal layers creates different Na<sup>+</sup> environments and structural disordering, leading to a reorganization of the ions in the unit cell.<span><sup><a href="#anie201811882-bib-0016e" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0016e_R_d8118046e789" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16e - Electrochem. Commun.">16e</a></sup></span>–<span><sup><a href="#anie201811882-bib-0016g" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0016g_R_d8118046e791" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16g - J. Power Sources">16g</a></sup></span> The dissimilar stacking of the two phases is revealed by HAADF and ABF images (Figure <a href="#anie201811882-fig-0003">3</a> a,b). The distinct crystal and atomic arrangement in the interface are obvious. The typical molecular models (inset) coupled with fast Fourier transformation (FFT) are also highly consistent with the P3 and P2 phases (Figure <a href="#anie201811882-fig-0003">3</a> c,d). The valence variation of Mn and Cu in the two different phases during charge and discharge is illustrated in the Supporting Information, Figure S6. The peaks of Cu-L<sub>3</sub> with a shift of 1.3 eV in both surface and interior indicate Cu shares the same redox reaction in P2 and P3 phase. However, the valence of Mn in the surface remains unchanged, indicating Mn in P3 phase does not participate in the reactions.<span><sup><a href="#anie201811882-bib-0016d" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0016d_R_d8118046e800" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16d - Microsc. Microanal.">16d</a></sup></span>,<span><sup><a href="#anie201811882-bib-0016h" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0016h_R_d8118046e802" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16h - Nat. Commun.">16h</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie201811882-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/73663d6d-b77b-41ba-9783-7bdbe6840eef/anie201811882-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/73663d6d-b77b-41ba-9783-7bdbe6840eef/anie201811882-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3489669d-7c8b-4ba4-886c-b800260f4fc4/anie201811882-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/73663d6d-b77b-41ba-9783-7bdbe6840eef/anie201811882-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie201811882-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.201811882" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) HAADF and b) ABF images of P2@P3 NCZM composite; the red and orange rectangles represent P3 structure and P2 structure areas, respectively. The enlarged images corresponding to the rectangle areas are shown in (c) and (d) with FFT patterns and molecular model structures as inset images.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The electrochemical performances of NCZM and NCM composites are shown in Figure <a href="#anie201811882-fig-0004">4</a>. Figure <a href="#anie201811882-fig-0004">4</a> a presents the CV curves of NCZM and NCM between 2.5–4.1 V. The pair of oxidation/reduction peaks of NCZM at 3.65 and 3.53 V corresponds to the redox couple of Cu<sup>2+</sup>/Cu<sup>3+</sup>, and another smaller pair of peaks from NCZM at 3.93 and 3.87 V can be attributed to the unusual structural change. The new pair of peaks of NCZM as compared with NCM sample can be clearly observed at 3.71 and 3.64 V, and might be related to the Cu<sup>2+</sup>/Cu<sup>3+</sup> redox couple influenced by the interaction of copper and manganese.<span><sup><a href="#anie201811882-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0011_R_d8118046e840" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chin. Phys. B">11</a>-<a href="#anie201811882-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0013_R_d8118046e840" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Mater. Chem. A">13</a></sup></span> Furthermore, the minor redox peaks below 3.2 V are attributable to a small quantity of Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie201811882-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0012_R_d8118046e846" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Sci.">12</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie201811882-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4b8cc747-770b-42c7-ae5e-e3469ad6e42a/anie201811882-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4b8cc747-770b-42c7-ae5e-e3469ad6e42a/anie201811882-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/42959bbb-abc3-4903-8d9c-7725a1d0a8d3/anie201811882-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4b8cc747-770b-42c7-ae5e-e3469ad6e42a/anie201811882-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie201811882-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.201811882" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) Cyclic voltammogram (CV) of NCZM and NCM. b) Rate capability at different rates of 0.1C, 0.2C, 0.5C, 1C, 2C, and 5C for NCZM (red) and NCM (blue). c) Cycling performance of NCZM (red) and NCM (blue). d) and e) The corresponding charge/discharge profiles of NCZM and NCM, respectively. f) Cycling performance of NCZM at different operating temperatures (50 and 60 °C).</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The excellent rate capability with subsequent cycling stability of this cathode material is demonstrated in Figure <a href="#anie201811882-fig-0004">4</a> b. The reversible discharge capacities of NCZM electrode are 88, 87.7, 85.5, 83.1, 80.2, and 73 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at current rates from 0.1C to 5C. In contrast, only 42.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> capacity remained for the NCM electrode cycled at 5C. Upon reverting back to a current rate of 0.1C, the capacity of NCZM was fully restored (87.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), which was not the case for NCM. The excellent rate performance might be related to the direct sodium diffusion paths in NCZM, which is in good agreement with the high-rate CV and corresponding high-rate cycling test (Supporting Information, Figure S7). The cycling capabilities of the NCZM and the NCM composite at 1C are shown in Figure <a href="#anie201811882-fig-0004">4</a> c, and the corresponding charge–discharge profiles at different cycles are displayed in Figure <a href="#anie201811882-fig-0004">4</a> d and e. The NCZM composite, which has a higher working voltage and lower polarization, initially delivers a discharge capacity of 84 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and a capacity retention of 85 % after 200 cycles. However, the discharge plateau of NCM around 3.9 V only last for 100 cycles, indicating a poor reversibility mechanism. The cycling stability of the NCZM electrode at high temperatures is shown in Figure <a href="#anie201811882-fig-0004">4</a> f. It shows a stable capacity of 78 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 50 °C for 165 cycles, of which 52.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> is retained at 60 °C for another 165 cycles. Its structure and electrochemical performance are maintained even when soaked in water (Supporting Information, Figure S8).</p>
         
         <p>In situ synchrotron XRD measurements were carried out to properly understand the structural evolution of NCZM (Figure <a href="#anie201811882-fig-0005">5</a> a). In the region of 0.51&lt;<i>x</i>&lt;0.78 (<i>x</i> representing the amount of Na in NCZM, which was calculated according to the specific capacity), the 002 peak of the initial phase, referred to as P2<sub>(original)</sub>, subtly decreases in 2<i>θ</i> value and intensity indicating a solid solution reaction with the oxidation of Mn and Cu (Figure <a href="#anie201811882-fig-0005">5</a> b and c). Meanwhile, a new phase starts to form and grow at the expense of P2<sub>(original)</sub> when charged to 3.8 V corresponding to the region of 0.42&lt;<i>x</i>&lt;0.51. Thus, the voltage slope from 3.8 to 4.1 V in this region can be attributed to the phase transition. The Cu L<sub>2,3</sub> peak barely shifts after 3.8 V is reached, indicating a large amount of Cu<sup>2+</sup> has already been oxidized below 3.8 V.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie201811882-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4d8cf09e-1266-46b1-b3f5-de14b1592334/anie201811882-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4d8cf09e-1266-46b1-b3f5-de14b1592334/anie201811882-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9e4659cb-9e6c-46d3-89f2-9246a0114fa2/anie201811882-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4d8cf09e-1266-46b1-b3f5-de14b1592334/anie201811882-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie201811882-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.201811882" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) Selected 2 <i>τ</i> regions of in situ synchrotron XRD data, highlighting the evolution of the 002, 004, 100, and 103 reflections and the corresponding potential profile (right). b),c) EELS spectra of Mn L<sub>2,3</sub> and Cu L<sub>2,3</sub> edges at different charge and discharge state. d),e) Enlarged images corresponding to the rectangle areas of 002 and 004 reflections, respectively. f),g) Corresponding ABF-STEM images of the interface, as reflected by the areas marked by the rectangles in the potential profile.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Enlarged images corresponding to the rectangle areas are provided in Figure <a href="#anie201811882-fig-0005">5</a> d and e. These new peaks with high intensity and sharp profiles are quite different from the so-called Z or OP4 phase (which is highly disordered), and can be assigned to the P2<sub>(new)</sub> phase.<span><sup><a href="#anie201811882-bib-0005b" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0005b_R_d8118046e951" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5b - Chem. Mater.">5b</a></sup></span>,<span><sup><a href="#anie201811882-bib-0005c" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0005c_R_d8118046e953" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5c - Energy Environ. Sci.">5c</a>, <a href="#anie201811882-bib-0016i" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie201811882-bib-0016i_R_d8118046e953" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16i - Inorg. Chem.">16i</a></sup></span> This illustrates that the NCZM inhibits the formation of the highly disordered phase up to 4.1 V. Furthermore, the clear interface between the two phases is still obvious during both the charge and discharge state, indicating the structural stability of the NCZM composite (Figure <a href="#anie201811882-fig-0005">5</a> f,g). The sodium ion diffusion coefficients obtained by GITT are shown in the Supporting Information, Figure S9. The diffusion coefficients of NCZM are in the range of 4.6×10<sup>−13</sup>–4.7×10<sup>−12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, which is twice that of NCM, indicating the Zn doping in this compound with increased unit cell volume are related to easier sodium ion diffusion.</p>
         
         <p>In summary, a novel hydrostable, Na<sub>0.78</sub>Cu<sub>0.27</sub>Zn<sub>0.06</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, with P2@P3 biphase structure was synthesized via a simple sol–gel method. Zn doping not only induced the formation of P3 phase but also offered a reversible mechanism, leading to improvements in both structural/electrochemical stability and humidity resistance. Moreover, electrochemical reactions based on the Cu<sup>2+/3+</sup> redox couple in the engineered material effectively decrease the fabrication cost of providing reliable reversible capability in the meantime. In this material, we demonstrated that most of Cu<sup>2+</sup> have been oxidized to Cu<sup>3+</sup> below 3.8 V during charge process with a solid solution reaction, and small quantities of Cu<sup>2+</sup> that are involved in the Na extraction upon 3.8 V with phase transitions. This biphasic material with high structural/electrochemical stability and tolerance toward water and temperature should encourage the tailoring of physical and chemical characteristics in copper-related biphasic composite.</p>
         
         <div class="article-section__content">
